In this paper we study, from analytical and geometric perspectives, the problem of adversarial learning in multiclass classification. By multiclass classification we mean the task of assigning classes i^^𝑖\hat{i} in a set of K𝐾K available classes to all inputs x^^𝑥\hat{x} in some feature space 𝒳𝒳{\mathcal{X}} based on the observation of training pairs z=(x,i)𝑧𝑥𝑖z=(x,i). The adversarial component of the problem refers to the desire of producing classification rules that are robust to data perturbations. Mathematically speaking, this means studying optimization problems of the form:(1.1)inff∈ℱsupμ~∈𝒫​(𝒵){R​(f,μ~)−C​(μ,μ~)}.subscriptinfimum𝑓ℱsubscriptsupremum~𝜇𝒫𝒵𝑅𝑓~𝜇𝐶𝜇~𝜇\inf_{f\in\mathcal{F}}\sup_{\widetilde{\mu}\in\mathcal{P}(\mathcal{Z})}\left\{R(f,\widetilde{\mu})-C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu})\right\}.Here, ℱℱ\mathcal{F} denotes the set of all probabilistic multiclass classifiers —see section 2; μ𝜇\mu denotes the observed data distribution, which in general is some probability measure on the space 𝒵=𝒳×{1,…,K}𝒵𝒳1…𝐾\mathcal{Z}={\mathcal{X}}\times\{1,\dots,K\}, but which for simplicity can be thought of as an empirical measure associated to a finite training data set; C𝐶C represents a notion of “distance” between data distributions; R​(f,μ~)𝑅𝑓~𝜇R(f,\widetilde{\mu}) is a risk functional relative to a data distribution μ~~𝜇\widetilde{\mu} (thought of as a perturbation of μ𝜇\mu) and a choice of loss function, which in this paper will be restricted to be the 00-111 loss. Problem (1.1) can be interpreted as a game between a learner and an adversary: the learner’s goal is to find a classifier with small risk, while the adversary tries to find a data perturbation μ~~𝜇\widetilde{\mu} that makes the risk for the learner large. The adversary has an implicit budget to perform their actions: the adversary can not choose a μ~~𝜇\widetilde{\mu} that is too far away (relative to C𝐶C) from the original data distribution μ𝜇\mu.

For a large family of functionals C𝐶C in (1.1) we show that the adversarial problem (1.1) is equivalent to a multimarginal optimal transport problem (MOT) of the form:(1.2)infπ∈ΠK​(μ)∫𝐜​(z1,…,zK)​𝑑π​(z1,…,zK),subscriptinfimum𝜋subscriptΠ𝐾𝜇𝐜subscript𝑧1…subscript𝑧𝐾differential-d𝜋subscript𝑧1…subscript𝑧𝐾\inf_{\pi\in\Pi_{K}(\mu)}\int\mathbf{c}(z_{1},\dots,z_{K})d\pi(z_{1},\dots,z_{K}),where 𝐜𝐜\mathbf{c} is a cost function discussed in detail throughout the paper and ΠK​(μ)subscriptΠ𝐾𝜇\Pi_{K}(\mu) is a space of couplings specified in section 2.1. As part of this equivalence, we explicitly describe how to construct solutions to the original problem (1.1) from solutions to the problem (1.2) and its dual, offering in this way new computational strategies for solving problem (1.1). Since most algorithms for OT are primal-dual (i.e., they simultaneously search for solutions to both the primal OT problem and its dual), it is actually possible to construct a saddle solution (f∗,μ∗)superscript𝑓superscript𝜇(f^{*},\mu^{*}) for (1.1) by running one such OT algorithm. The equivalence between (1.1) and (1.2) that we study here is an extension to the multi-class case of a series of recent results connecting adversarial learning in binary classification with optimal transport: [BCM19, Nak19, PJ21a, PJ21b, GM22].

In order to establish the equivalence between (1.1) and (1.2), we develop another interesting equivalent reformulation of (1.1) that reveals a rich geometric structure of the original adversarial problem. This reformulation takes the form of a generalized barycenter probleminfλ,μ~1,…,μ~Kλ​(𝒳)+∑i∈[K]C​(μi,μ~i)s.t.​λ≥μ~i,i∈[K],formulae-sequencesubscriptinfimum𝜆subscript~𝜇1…subscript~𝜇𝐾𝜆𝒳subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾𝐶subscript𝜇𝑖subscript~𝜇𝑖s.t.𝜆subscript~𝜇𝑖𝑖delimited-[]𝐾\inf_{\lambda,\widetilde{\mu}_{1},\ldots,\widetilde{\mu}_{K}}\lambda(\mathcal{X})+\sum_{i\in[K]}C(\mu_{i},\widetilde{\mu}_{i})\quad\textup{s.t.}\;\lambda\geq\widetilde{\mu}_{i},i\in[K],which is a novel variant of the Wasserstein barycenter problemsintroduced in [AC11, CE10].In the classical Wasserstein barycenter problem, given K𝐾K probability measures ϱ1,…,ϱKsubscriptitalic-ϱ1…subscriptitalic-ϱ𝐾\varrho_{1},\ldots,\varrho_{K} defined over a Polish space 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X} and a cost c:𝒳×𝒳→[0,∞]:𝑐→𝒳𝒳0c:\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{X}\to[0,\infty], one tries to find a probability measure ϱitalic-ϱ\varrho such that the summed cost of transporting each of the ϱisubscriptitalic-ϱ𝑖\varrho_{i} onto ϱitalic-ϱ\varrho is as small as possible. In our generalized problem, we try to find a nonnegative measure λ𝜆\lambda (no longer necessarily a probability measure) such that the total mass of λ𝜆\lambda plus the summed cost of transporting each μisubscript𝜇𝑖\mu_{i} (not necessarily having the same total mass) onto some part of λ𝜆\lambda is as small as possible. Here transporting a μisubscript𝜇𝑖\mu_{i} onto some part of λ𝜆\lambda means we want to find a measure μ~i≤λsubscript~𝜇𝑖𝜆\widetilde{\mu}_{i}\leq\lambda and transport μisubscript𝜇𝑖\mu_{i} to μ~isubscript~𝜇𝑖\widetilde{\mu}_{i} in the classical optimal transport sense. This problem will be studied in detail in section 3. We prove that these generalized barycenter problems can be written as appropriate MOT problems, a result that is analogous to ones in [AC11, CE10] for standard Wasserstein barycenter problems.

From the equivalence with the generalized barycenter problem we will be able to deduce that optimal adversarial attacks can always be obtained as suitable barycenters of K𝐾K or less points in the original training data set. Also, from this reformulation we will be able to recognize the structure of the cost function 𝐜𝐜\mathbf{c} in (1.2): for the adversary to obtain their optimal strategy, they can actually localize their problem to sets of K𝐾K or fewer data points —see section 2.1. Other theoretical, methodological, and computational implications of these reformulations will be pursued in future work. See section 6 for a discussion on future directions for research.

In contrast to many of the existing applications of OT to ML, it is worth emphasizing that in this work OT arises naturally in connection with a learning problem, rather than as a particular way to address a certain machine learning task. For the growing literature in multimarginal optimal transportation this paper offers new examples of cost functions worthy of study. MOT is a rich topic that has been developed over the years from theoretical and applied perspectives. After the first mathematical analysis of general MOT problems in [GS98], there have been numerous subsequent papers establishing geometric and analytic results (e.g., [KP13, Pas15, KP15, CMP17]) for MOT problems. MOT problems have also been used extensively in applications. For example, they appear in the so-called density functional theory in physics [SGGS07, BDPGG12, CFK13, ML13, CDPDM15], and in economics [Eke05, CMN10, CE10]. In the machine learning community, researchers have recently explored many interesting applications, including generative adversarial networks(GANs) [CCK+18, CMZ+19] and Wasserstein Barycenters [AC11, CD14, BCC+15, COO15, SLD18, DD20], where MOTs are used. Recent works like [DMG20, HRCK21] develop a connection between the Schrödinger bridge problem and MOT. MOT problems have been extended to the unbalanced setting —see [BvLNS21].

The rest of the paper is organized as follows. In section 2, we introduce most mathematical objects and notation used throughout the rest of the paper. We also introduce the generalized Wasserstein barycenter problem, which can be interpreted as dual of the original adversarial problem (1.1), and define in detail the MOT problem (1.2). In section 3, we study the aforementioned generalized Wasserstein barycenter problem and prove its equivalence with 1) a stratified barycenter problem and 2) a first version of an MOT problem. In section 4 we discuss the equivalence between (1.1) and (1.2) through the duality results in earlier sections. In section 5, we present a collection of examples and numerical experiments whose goal is to illustrate the theory developed throughout the paper and provide further insights into the geometric structure of adversarial learning in multiclass classification. Finally, we wrap-up the paper in section 6 by presenting some conclusions and discussing some future directions for research.

Throughout the paper (𝒳,d)𝒳𝑑(\mathcal{X},d) will be a Polish space, [K]:={1,…,K}assigndelimited-[]𝐾1…𝐾[K]:=\{1,\dots,K\} with K≥2𝐾2K\geq 2, and 𝒵:=𝒳×[K]assign𝒵𝒳delimited-[]𝐾\mathcal{Z}:=\mathcal{X}\times[K]. We regard 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X} as the feature space of our learning problem and [K]delimited-[]𝐾[K] as the set of classes or labels.

Let μ𝜇\mu be a finite positive Borel measure (not necessarily a probability measure) over 𝒵𝒵\mathcal{Z}. Given i∈[K]𝑖delimited-[]𝐾i\in[K], we use μisubscript𝜇𝑖\mu_{i} to represent the positive measure over 𝒳𝒳{\mathcal{X}} defined as(2.1)μi​(A):=μ​(A×{i}),assignsubscript𝜇𝑖𝐴𝜇𝐴𝑖\mu_{i}(A):=\mu\left(A\times\{i\}\right),for all measurable subsets A𝐴A of 𝒳𝒳{\mathcal{X}}. In the sequel, we use μ𝜇\mu to represent a fixed data distribution, which we regard as an observed data distribution or training data distribution, and use μ~~𝜇\widetilde{\mu} to represent any other arbitrary finite positive measure over 𝒵𝒵\mathcal{Z}. Through this paper we use ℳ+​(𝒳)subscriptℳ𝒳\mathcal{M}_{+}(\mathcal{X}) and ℳ+​(𝒵)subscriptℳ𝒵\mathcal{M}_{+}(\mathcal{Z}) to denote the set of finite positive (Borel) measures over 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X} and 𝒵𝒵\mathcal{Z}, respectively.

Through this paper, the cost function in (1.1) will take the form:C​(μ,μ~):=minπ∈Γ​(μ,μ~)​∫c𝒵​(z,z~)​𝑑π​(z,z~),assign𝐶𝜇~𝜇subscript𝜋Γ𝜇~𝜇subscript𝑐𝒵𝑧~𝑧differential-d𝜋𝑧~𝑧C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu}):=\min_{\pi\in\Gamma(\mu,\widetilde{\mu})}\int c_{\mathcal{Z}}(z,\tilde{z})d\pi(z,\tilde{z}),for some cost function c𝒵:𝒵×𝒵→[0,∞]:subscript𝑐𝒵→𝒵𝒵0c_{\mathcal{Z}}:\mathcal{Z}\times\mathcal{Z}\rightarrow[0,\infty]. Here and in the remainder of the paper the set Γ​(⋅,⋅)Γ⋅⋅\Gamma(\cdot,\cdot) represents the set of couplings between two positive measures over the same space; for example, Γ​(μ,μ~)Γ𝜇~𝜇\Gamma(\mu,\widetilde{\mu}) denotes the set of positive measures over 𝒵×𝒵𝒵𝒵\mathcal{Z}\times\mathcal{Z} with first marginal equal to μ𝜇\mu and second marginal equal to μ~~𝜇\widetilde{\mu}.

The structure of c𝒵subscript𝑐𝒵c_{\mathcal{Z}} described in Assumption 2.1 is standard in the literature of adversarial learning and can be motivated by the fact that in many applications of interest it is natural to think that the “true” label associated to a perturbation x~~𝑥\tilde{x} of a data point x𝑥x coincides with the true label of the original x𝑥x. Naturally, this is simply a modeling choice, and other cost structures of interest can be studied elsewhere. The lower semicontinuity and compactness assumptions on c𝑐c are technical requirements that we use in the remainder. All cost functions of interest satisfy these properties —see the examples below.

If we decompose μ𝜇\mu and μ~~𝜇\widetilde{\mu} into measures μi,μ~isubscript𝜇𝑖subscript~𝜇𝑖\mu_{i},\widetilde{\mu}_{i} as in (2.1), it is possible to write C​(μ,μ~)𝐶𝜇~𝜇C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu}) asC​(μ,μ~)=∑i∈[K]C​(μi,μ~i),𝐶𝜇~𝜇subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾𝐶subscript𝜇𝑖subscript~𝜇𝑖C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu})=\sum_{i\in[K]}C(\mu_{i},\widetilde{\mu}_{i}),abusing notation slightly and interpreting C​(μi,μ~i)𝐶subscript𝜇𝑖subscript~𝜇𝑖C(\mu_{i},\widetilde{\mu}_{i}) as

(2.2)C​(μi,μ~i)=minπ∈Γ​(μi,μ~i)​∫c​(x,x~)​𝑑π​(x,x~).𝐶subscript𝜇𝑖subscript~𝜇𝑖subscript𝜋Γsubscript𝜇𝑖subscript~𝜇𝑖𝑐𝑥~𝑥differential-d𝜋𝑥~𝑥C(\mu_{i},\widetilde{\mu}_{i})=\min_{\pi\in\Gamma(\mu_{i},\widetilde{\mu}_{i})}\int c(x,\tilde{x})d\pi(x,\tilde{x}).

We introduce two notions that will be used throughout our analysis. The solution space ℱℱ\mathcal{F} in (1.1) is the full set of weak partitions, or probabilistic classifiers, defined byℱ:={f:𝒳→Δ[K]:f​ Borel measurable },assignℱconditional-set𝑓:→𝒳subscriptΔdelimited-[]𝐾𝑓 Borel measurable \mathcal{F}:=\left\{f:\mathcal{X}\to\Delta_{[K]}:f\text{ Borel measurable }\right\},whereΔ[K]:={(ui)i∈[K]:0≤ui≤1,∑i∈[K]ui=1},assignsubscriptΔdelimited-[]𝐾conditional-setsubscriptsubscript𝑢𝑖𝑖delimited-[]𝐾formulae-sequence0subscript𝑢𝑖1subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾subscript𝑢𝑖1\Delta_{[K]}:=\left\{(u_{i})_{i\in[K]}:0\leq u_{i}\leq 1,\sum_{i\in[K]}u_{i}=1\right\},i.e., ΔKsubscriptΔ𝐾\Delta_{K} is the set of probability distributions over [K]delimited-[]𝐾[K]. In other words, at each x∈𝒳𝑥𝒳x\in\mathcal{X}, f​(x)𝑓𝑥f(x) is a probability distribution over [K]delimited-[]𝐾[K] representing the likelihood, according to the specific classifier f𝑓f chosen by the learner, that a given x𝑥x belongs to any of the available classes. Probabilistic classifiers are widely used in applications as they allow for the use of standard optimization techniques when training models. We want to highlight that the f𝑓fs in ℱℱ\mathcal{F} are only assumed to be Borel measurable. This means that the learner in problem (1.1) can be considered to be agnostic to any specific model for the classifiers and in that sense (1.1) can be interpreted as a robust generalization of the notion of Bayes classifier studied in statistical learning.

For a given u∈Δ[K]𝑢subscriptΔdelimited-[]𝐾u\in\Delta_{[K]} and a given i∈[K]𝑖delimited-[]𝐾i\in[K], we define the loss:ℓ​(u,i):=1−ui.assignℓ𝑢𝑖1subscript𝑢𝑖\ell(u,i):=1-u_{i}.Notice that ℓ​(ej,i)ℓsubscript𝑒𝑗𝑖\ell(e_{j},i) is equal to 111 if i≠j𝑖𝑗i\not=j and 00 if i=j𝑖𝑗i=j. ℓℓ\ell thus extends the 00-111 loss to weak classifiers, and from now on we will refer to it simply as the 00-111 loss. Combining the above with the structure of the cost C​(μ,μ~)𝐶𝜇~𝜇C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu}), we conclude that problem (2.7) is equivalent to the generalized barycenter problem mentioned in the introduction:(2.8)Bμ∗:=infλ,μ~1,…,μ~K{λ​(𝒳)+∑i∈[K]C​(μi,μ~i): λ≥μ~i for all i∈[K]},assignsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝜇subscriptinfimum𝜆subscript~𝜇1…subscript~𝜇𝐾conditional-set𝜆𝒳subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾𝐶subscript𝜇𝑖subscript~𝜇𝑖 λ≥μ~i for all i∈[K]B_{\mu}^{*}:=\inf_{\lambda,\widetilde{\mu}_{1},\ldots,\widetilde{\mu}_{K}}\left\{\lambda(\mathcal{X})+\sum_{i\in[K]}C(\mu_{i},\widetilde{\mu}_{i}):\text{ $\lambda\geq\widetilde{\mu}_{i}$ for all $i\in[K]$}\right\},where we use the notation Bμ∗superscriptsubscript𝐵𝜇B_{\mu}^{*} for future reference; see Figure 1 for a pictorial explanation.

Before providing the details of our MOT problem (1.2), it is worth introducing the generic MOT problem first. Let 𝒮1,…,𝒮Ksubscript𝒮1…subscript𝒮𝐾\mathcal{S}_{1},\dots,\mathcal{S}_{K} be fixed spaces and let 𝐜:𝒮1×⋯×𝒮K→ℝ∪{+∞,−∞}:𝐜→subscript𝒮1⋯subscript𝒮𝐾ℝ\mathbf{c}:\mathcal{S}_{1}\times\dots\times\mathcal{S}_{K}\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{+\infty,-\infty\} be a cost function. For each 1≤k≤K1𝑘𝐾1\leq k\leq K, let νk∈𝒫​(𝒮k)subscript𝜈𝑘𝒫subscript𝒮𝑘\nu_{k}\in\mathcal{P}(\mathcal{S}_{k}) be a Borel probability measure. One of the most popular examples of MOT problems is connected to the Wasserstein Barycenter problem over 𝒫​(𝒳)𝒫𝒳\mathcal{P}(\mathcal{X}); see [Eke05, CMN10, AC11]. Let c:𝒳×𝒳→ℝ∪{+∞,−∞}:𝑐→𝒳𝒳ℝc:\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{X}\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{+\infty,-\infty\} be a fixed pairwise cost function. In the Wasserstein barycenter problem the goal is to find a solution ν∗superscript𝜈\nu^{*} to the probleminfν′∑i∈[K]C​(ν′,νi)where ​C​(ν,νi):=infπ∈Π​(ν,νi)∫𝒳×𝒳c​(x′,x)​𝑑π​(x′,x).assignsubscriptinfimumsuperscript𝜈′subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾𝐶superscript𝜈′subscript𝜈𝑖where 𝐶𝜈subscript𝜈𝑖subscriptinfimum𝜋Π𝜈subscript𝜈𝑖subscript𝒳𝒳𝑐superscript𝑥′𝑥differential-d𝜋superscript𝑥′𝑥\inf_{\nu^{\prime}}\sum_{i\in[K]}C(\nu^{\prime},\nu_{i})\quad\text{where }C(\nu,\nu_{i}):=\inf_{\pi\in\Pi(\nu,\nu_{i})}\int_{\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{X}}c(x^{\prime},x)d\pi(x^{\prime},x).Such ν∗superscript𝜈\nu^{*} can be interpreted as an “average” or barycenter of the input measures ν1,…,νKsubscript𝜈1…subscript𝜈𝐾\nu_{1},\dots,\nu_{K} relative to the cost C𝐶C. It can then be showed that the above Wasserstein barycenter problem is equivalent to solving the following MOT probleminfπ∈Π​(ν1,…,νK)∫𝒳K𝐜​(x1,…,xK)​𝑑π​(x1,…,xK),subscriptinfimum𝜋Πsubscript𝜈1…subscript𝜈𝐾subscriptsuperscript𝒳𝐾𝐜subscript𝑥1…subscript𝑥𝐾differential-d𝜋subscript𝑥1…subscript𝑥𝐾\inf_{\pi\in\Pi(\nu_{1},\dots,\nu_{K})}\int_{\mathcal{X}^{K}}\mathbf{c}(x_{1},\dots,x_{K})d\pi(x_{1},\dots,x_{K}),where𝐜​(x1,…,xK):=infx′∈𝒳∑i∈[K]c​(x′,xi).assign𝐜subscript𝑥1…subscript𝑥𝐾subscriptinfimumsuperscript𝑥′𝒳subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾𝑐superscript𝑥′subscript𝑥𝑖\mathbf{c}(x_{1},\dots,x_{K}):=\inf_{x^{\prime}\in\mathcal{X}}\sum_{i\in[K]}c(x^{\prime},x_{i}).Indeed, let π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*} be a minimizer of the above MOT problem. Defining ν∗=T#​πsuperscript𝜈subscript𝑇#𝜋\nu^{*}=T_{\#}\pi, where T​(x1,…,xK):=argminx′​∑i∈[K]c​(x′,xi)assign𝑇subscript𝑥1…subscript𝑥𝐾subscriptargminsuperscript𝑥′subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾𝑐superscript𝑥′subscript𝑥𝑖T(x_{1},\dots,x_{K}):=\operatorname*{argmin}_{x^{\prime}}\sum_{i\in[K]}c(x^{\prime},x_{i}), i.e., defining ν∗superscript𝜈\nu^{*} as the pushforward measure of π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*} with respect to the barycenter mapping T𝑇T, one can recover a solution to the original barycenter problem. Conversely, one can use a Wasserstein barycenter ν∗superscript𝜈\nu^{*} and couplings πisubscript𝜋𝑖\pi_{i} realizing the costs C​(ν∗,νi)𝐶superscript𝜈subscript𝜈𝑖C(\nu^{*},\nu_{i}) to build a solution to the MOT problem; see more details in [AC11].

Now we are ready to state problem (1.2) precisely. For this, we will need to modify the space 𝒵𝒵\mathcal{Z} and in particular add an extra element to it that will be denoted by the symbol \mathghost\mathghost\mathghost. The marginals of the couplings in the desired MOT problem will be probability measures over the set 𝒵∗:=𝒵∪{\mathghost}assignsubscript𝒵𝒵\mathghost\mathcal{Z}_{*}:=\mathcal{Z}\cup\{\mathghost\}. More precisely, letting Pisubscript𝑃𝑖P_{i} represent the projection onto the i𝑖i-th coordinate, we consider the set:(2.10)ΠK(μ):={π∈𝒫(𝒵∗K):Pi​♯π=12​μ​(𝒵)μ(⋅∩𝒵)+12δ\mathghost for all i∈[K]}.\Pi_{K}(\mu):=\left\{\pi\in\mathcal{P}(\mathcal{Z}_{*}^{K})\>:\>P_{i\sharp}\pi=\frac{1}{2\mu(\mathcal{Z})}\mu(\cdot\cap\mathcal{Z})+\frac{1}{2}\delta_{\mathghost}\text{ for all }i\in[K]\right\}.Notice that in this set all K𝐾K marginals are the same. Dividing by the factor 12​μ​(𝒵)12𝜇𝒵\frac{1}{2\mu(\mathcal{Z})}, the set ΠK​(μ)subscriptΠ𝐾𝜇\Pi_{K}(\mu) is made to be consistent with the literature on multimarginal optimal transport, where sets of couplings are typically assumed to be probability measures.

Let us now discuss the cost function for the desired MOT problem. For a given tuple (z1,…,zK)subscript𝑧1…subscript𝑧𝐾(z_{1},\dots,z_{K}) in 𝒵∗Ksuperscriptsubscript𝒵𝐾\mathcal{Z}_{*}^{K}, often denoted by z→→𝑧\vec{z} in the sequel for convenience, we define(2.11)𝐜​(z1,…,zK):=Bμ^z→∗,assign𝐜subscript𝑧1…subscript𝑧𝐾subscriptsuperscript𝐵subscript^𝜇→𝑧\mathbf{c}(z_{1},\dots,z_{K}):=B^{*}_{\widehat{\mu}_{\vec{z}}},where μ^z→subscript^𝜇→𝑧\widehat{\mu}_{\vec{z}} is the positive measure (not necessarily a probability measure) defined as:μ^z→:=1K​∑l​ s.t. ​zl≠\mathghostKδzl.assignsubscript^𝜇→𝑧1𝐾superscriptsubscript𝑙 s.t. subscript𝑧𝑙\mathghost𝐾subscript𝛿subscript𝑧𝑙\widehat{\mu}_{\vec{z}}:=\frac{1}{K}\sum_{l\text{ s.t. }z_{l}\not=\mathghost}^{K}\delta_{z_{l}}.Recall that Bμ^z→∗subscriptsuperscript𝐵subscript^𝜇→𝑧B^{*}_{\widehat{\mu}_{\vec{z}}} is equal to (2.8) (alternatively, equal to (2.7)) when μ𝜇\mu is equal to μ^z→subscript^𝜇→𝑧{\widehat{\mu}_{\vec{z}}}. In this sense, 𝐜​(z1,…,zK)𝐜subscript𝑧1…subscript𝑧𝐾\mathbf{c}(z_{1},\dots,z_{K}) of (2.11) is the value of the generalized barycenter problem given μ^z→subscript^𝜇→𝑧\widehat{\mu}_{\vec{z}} as the data distribution, or local generalized barycenter problem.

Following the literature of MOT, the dual of our MOT problem can be written as(2.12)supϕ∈Φ{∑j=1K∫𝒳×[K]ϕj​(zj)​12​μ​(𝒵)​𝑑μ​(zj)+12​∑j=1Kϕj​(\mathghost)},subscriptsupremumitalic-ϕΦsuperscriptsubscript𝑗1𝐾subscript𝒳delimited-[]𝐾subscriptitalic-ϕ𝑗subscript𝑧𝑗12𝜇𝒵differential-d𝜇subscript𝑧𝑗12superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝐾subscriptitalic-ϕ𝑗\mathghost\sup_{\phi\in\Phi}\left\{\sum_{j=1}^{K}\int_{\mathcal{X}\times[K]}\phi_{j}(z_{j})\frac{1}{2\mu(\mathcal{Z})}d\mu(z_{j})+\frac{1}{2}\sum_{j=1}^{K}\phi_{j}(\mathghost)\right\},where(2.13)Φ:={ϕ=(ϕ1,…,ϕK)∈∏j=1KL1​(12​μ​(𝒵)​μ+12​δ\mathghost):∑j=1Kϕj​(zj)≤Bμ^z→∗,∀z→∈𝒵∗K}.assignΦconditional-setitalic-ϕsubscriptitalic-ϕ1…subscriptitalic-ϕ𝐾superscriptsubscriptproduct𝑗1𝐾superscript𝐿112𝜇𝒵𝜇12subscript𝛿\mathghostformulae-sequencesuperscriptsubscript𝑗1𝐾subscriptitalic-ϕ𝑗subscript𝑧𝑗subscriptsuperscript𝐵subscript^𝜇→𝑧for-all→𝑧superscriptsubscript𝒵𝐾\Phi:=\left\{\phi=(\phi_{1},\dots,\phi_{K})\in\prod_{j=1}^{K}L^{1}\big{(}\frac{1}{2\mu(\mathcal{Z})}\mu+\frac{1}{2}\delta_{\mathghost}\big{)}:\sum_{j=1}^{K}\phi_{j}(z_{j})\leq B^{*}_{\widehat{\mu}_{\vec{z}}},\quad\forall\vec{z}\in\mathcal{Z}_{*}^{K}\right\}.We will later show that under Assumption 2.1 there is no duality gap between the MOT problem and its dual (2.12) —see Corollary 4.5.

One of the main results of the paper is the following.

One immediate consequence of Theorem 2.8 is that with the identityRμ∗=μ​(𝒵)−B~μ∗,subscriptsuperscript𝑅𝜇𝜇𝒵subscriptsuperscript~𝐵𝜇R^{*}_{\mu}=\mu(\mathcal{Z})-\widetilde{B}^{*}_{\mu},one can compute Rμ∗subscriptsuperscript𝑅𝜇R^{*}_{\mu}, the optimal adversarial risk, by finding the optimal value of the equivalent MOT problem. To find the latter, one could attempt to use one of the off-the-shelf algorithms in computational optimal transport. Some algorithms to solve generic MOTs that have been developed recently include the ones proposed in see [BCC+15, BCN19, LHCJ22, TDGU20, HRCK21, ABA21, Car22]. Our numerical results for a subsample of MNIST and CIFAR 10, shown in Figure 6, are obtained using the algorithm discussed in [LHCJ22], also known as MOT Sinkhorn algorithm; see subsection 5.3 for more details. We want to warn the reader, however, that off-the-shelf MOT algorithms may suffer an excessive computational burden when K𝐾K goes beyond 444. For this reason, it is important to develop algorithms that exploit the structure of our MOT problem, which, as we will discuss below, has the structure of a generalized barycenter problem. An investigation on more specific algorithms is left for future work.

The proof of Theorem 2.8 is presented throughout section 4; the expression for (f∗,μ~∗)superscript𝑓superscript~𝜇(f^{*},\widetilde{\mu}^{*}) in terms of (ϕ∗,π∗)superscriptitalic-ϕsuperscript𝜋(\phi^{*},\pi^{*}) is presented in Corollary 4.7. Given the definition of the cost function 𝐜𝐜\mathbf{c}, Theorem 2.8 states that the adversarial problem localizes to data sets consisting of K𝐾K or less equally weighted points. More precisely, the problem for the adversary reduces to first determining their actions when facing arbitrary distributions supported on K𝐾K or fewer data points, and then finding an optimal grouping for the data in order to assemble their global strategy. The ghost element, \mathghost\mathghost\mathghost, indicates when fewer than K𝐾K points are being grouped by the adversary. We highlight that it is not always (globally) optimal for the adversary to group together points from all the K𝐾K different classes whenever it is possible.

We emphasize that from the solution to the MOT and its dual, one can directly obtain an optimal adversarial attack and an optimal classification rule for the original adversarial problem. Note that problem (1.2) is a problem solved by the adversary: ideally, the adversary wants to group together points (z1,…,zK)subscript𝑧1…subscript𝑧𝐾(z_{1},\dots,z_{K}) for which there is a low classification power Bμ^z→∗subscriptsuperscript𝐵subscript^𝜇→𝑧B^{*}_{\widehat{\mu}_{\vec{z}}} (or alternatively large robust risk). On the other hand, the dual of (1.2) can be interpreted as a maximization problem solved by the learner. We formalize this novel connection in subsection 4.3: see Corollary 4.7.

In order to prove Theorem 2.8, we will first obtain a series of equivalent reformulations of problem (2.8) which will reveal a rich geometric structure of the adversarial problem and will facilitate the connection with the desired MOT problem. These equivalent formulations are of interest in their own right.

We begin this section by proving that the generalized barycenter problem always has at least one solution. In the following subsections we will then discuss a series of equivalent problems to the generalized barycenter problem, their duals, and some geometric properties of their solutions.

We can reduce (2.8) to a more concrete problem by partitioning λ𝜆\lambda and each of μisubscript𝜇𝑖\mu_{i}’s properly, eliminating the variables μ~isubscript~𝜇𝑖\widetilde{\mu}_{i}’s from the optimization. We start with the following observation.

In the next proposition we will show that any feasible solution of problem (3.1) induces a feasible solution of (3.6) with lesser or equal value. This will prove the equivalence between problems (3.1) and (3.6) and will provide a powerful geometric characterization of optimal generalized barycenters.

We begin by noting that the marginal constraints on γAsubscript𝛾𝐴\gamma_{A} are compatible in the sense that for any g∈Cb​(𝒳)𝑔subscript𝐶𝑏𝒳g\in C_{b}(\mathcal{X}) and i∈A𝑖𝐴i\in A we have∫𝒳g​(x′)​𝑑γi,A​(xi,x′)=∫𝒳g​(x′)​𝑑λA​(x′).subscript𝒳𝑔superscript𝑥′differential-dsubscript𝛾𝑖𝐴subscript𝑥𝑖superscript𝑥′subscript𝒳𝑔superscript𝑥′differential-dsubscript𝜆𝐴superscript𝑥′\int_{\mathcal{X}}g(x^{\prime})d\gamma_{i,A}(x_{i},x^{\prime})=\int_{\mathcal{X}}g(x^{\prime})d\lambda_{A}(x^{\prime}).Thus, each γAsubscript𝛾𝐴\gamma_{A} is well-defined.

Note that in problem (3.6) we need to find a distribution πAsubscript𝜋𝐴\pi_{A} for each A∈SK𝐴subscript𝑆𝐾A\in S_{K}. In this section we discuss the dual problems of the different formulations of the generalized barycenter problem studied in section 3.1.

The derivation of the dual problems is standard.

We now want to show that the dual problems attain the same values as the original primal problems. We begin with a minimax lemma for the following partial optimal transport problem.

Using Lemma 3.17 we can prove that there is no duality gap for bounded and Lipschitz costs. We will then show that there is no duality gap for general costs by approximation.

If we fix some convex weakly compact subset ℬ⊂ℳ​(𝒳)ℬℳ𝒳\mathcal{B}\subset\mathcal{M}(\mathcal{X}) containing λ𝜆\lambda, then it follows from Lemma 3.17 and the optimality of λ𝜆\lambda that there exists φi∗,ψi∗superscriptsubscript𝜑𝑖superscriptsubscript𝜓𝑖\varphi_{i}^{*},\psi_{i}^{*} such that(3.15)λ​(𝒳)+∑i∈[K]C​(μi,μ~i)=minρ∈ℬ⁡ρ​(𝒳)+∑i∈[K]∫𝒳φi∗​(x)​𝑑μi​(x)−ψi∗​(x′)​d​ρ​(x′),𝜆𝒳subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾𝐶subscript𝜇𝑖subscript~𝜇𝑖subscript𝜌ℬ𝜌𝒳subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾subscript𝒳superscriptsubscript𝜑𝑖𝑥differential-dsubscript𝜇𝑖𝑥superscriptsubscript𝜓𝑖superscript𝑥′𝑑𝜌superscript𝑥′\lambda(\mathcal{X})+\sum_{i\in[K]}C(\mu_{i},\tilde{\mu}_{i})=\min_{\rho\in\mathcal{B}}\rho(\mathcal{X})+\sum_{i\in[K]}\int_{\mathcal{X}}\varphi_{i}^{*}(x)d\mu_{i}(x)-\psi_{i}^{*}(x^{\prime})d\rho(x^{\prime}),ψi∗​(x′)≥0superscriptsubscript𝜓𝑖superscript𝑥′0\psi_{i}^{*}(x^{\prime})\geq 0, and (φi∗)c¯​(x′)=ψi∗​(x′),(ψi∗)c​(x)=φi∗​(x)formulae-sequencesuperscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝜑𝑖¯𝑐superscript𝑥′superscriptsubscript𝜓𝑖superscript𝑥′superscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝜓𝑖𝑐𝑥superscriptsubscript𝜑𝑖𝑥(\varphi_{i}^{*})^{\bar{c}}(x^{\prime})=\psi_{i}^{*}(x^{\prime}),(\psi_{i}^{*})^{c}(x)=\varphi_{i}^{*}(x) for all 1≤i≤K1𝑖𝐾1\leq i\leq K and x,x′∈𝒳𝑥superscript𝑥′𝒳x,x^{\prime}\in\mathcal{X}.If there exists x′∈𝒳superscript𝑥′𝒳x^{\prime}\in\mathcal{X} such that ∑i∈[K]ψi∗​(x′)>1subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾superscriptsubscript𝜓𝑖superscript𝑥′1\sum_{i\in[K]}\psi_{i}^{*}(x^{\prime})>1, then we can make the right hand side of (3.15) smaller than the left hand side by choosing ρ=M​δx′𝜌𝑀subscript𝛿superscript𝑥′\rho=M\delta_{x^{\prime}} for some sufficiently large value of M𝑀M. Let η:[0,∞)→[0,∞):𝜂→00\eta:[0,\infty)\to[0,\infty) be a smooth strictly increasing function such that η​(x)=x𝜂𝑥𝑥\eta(x)=x for x≤1𝑥1x\leq 1 and η​(x)≤2𝜂𝑥2\eta(x)\leq 2 for all x∈[0,∞)𝑥0x\in[0,\infty).For each j∈ℤ+𝑗subscriptℤj\in\mathbb{Z}_{+}, definec~j​(x,x′):=inf(x1,x1′)∈𝒳×𝒳c​(x1,x1′)+j​d​(x,x1)+j​d​(x′,x1),assignsubscript~𝑐𝑗𝑥superscript𝑥′subscriptinfimumsubscript𝑥1superscriptsubscript𝑥1′𝒳𝒳𝑐subscript𝑥1superscriptsubscript𝑥1′𝑗𝑑𝑥subscript𝑥1𝑗𝑑superscript𝑥′subscript𝑥1\tilde{c}_{j}(x,x^{\prime}):=\inf_{(x_{1},x_{1}^{\prime})\in\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{X}}c(x_{1},x_{1}^{\prime})+jd(x,x_{1})+jd(x^{\prime},x_{1}),and cj​(x,x′):=j​η​(c~j​(x,x′)j)assignsubscript𝑐𝑗𝑥superscript𝑥′𝑗𝜂subscript~𝑐𝑗𝑥superscript𝑥′𝑗c_{j}(x,x^{\prime}):=j\eta(\frac{\tilde{c}_{j}(x,x^{\prime})}{j}). Since c𝑐c is lower semicontinuous it is straightforward to check that cjsubscript𝑐𝑗c_{j} converges to c𝑐c pointwise everywhere.

In this section, we prove Theorem 2.8 and return to the adversarial problem (1.1).

In summary, we have proved that for a given ϕ=(ϕ1,…,ϕK)∈Φitalic-ϕsubscriptitalic-ϕ1…subscriptitalic-ϕ𝐾Φ\phi=(\phi_{1},\dots,\phi_{K})\in\Phi, its associated (ψ1,…,ψK)subscript𝜓1…subscript𝜓𝐾(\psi_{1},\dots,\psi_{K}) (which satisfies (4.1)) is feasible for (3.13). From Proposition 4.3 it follows that the cost function 𝐜​(z1,…,zK)𝐜subscript𝑧1…subscript𝑧𝐾\mathbf{c}(z_{1},\dots,z_{K}) is lower semi-continuous on (𝒵∗K,d^K)\mathcal{Z}_{*}^{K},\widehat{d}_{K}), which is a Polish space. Applying Theorem 1.31.31.3 in [Vil03], which is stated for the usual optimal transport, but that can be generalized to the MOT setting, we obtain the desired duality. The existence of a minimizer π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*} follows from the lower semi-continuity of 𝐜​(z1,…,zK)𝐜subscript𝑧1…subscript𝑧𝐾\mathbf{c}(z_{1},\dots,z_{K}) and the compactness of ΠK​(μ)subscriptΠ𝐾𝜇\Pi_{K}(\mu).∎

First, we will verify that f∗superscript𝑓f^{*} is feasible for (3.12). By Proposition 4.2, ψ=(ψ1,…,ψK)𝜓subscript𝜓1…subscript𝜓𝐾\psi=(\psi_{1},\dots,\psi_{K}) is feasible for (3.13), whereψi​(x)=∑j=1Kϕj∗​(x,i)+∑j=1Kϕj∗​(\mathghost).subscript𝜓𝑖𝑥superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝐾subscriptsuperscriptitalic-ϕ𝑗𝑥𝑖superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝐾subscriptsuperscriptitalic-ϕ𝑗\mathghost\psi_{i}(x)=\sum_{j=1}^{K}\phi^{*}_{j}(x,i)+\sum_{j=1}^{K}\phi^{*}_{j}(\mathghost).Fix x~~𝑥\widetilde{x} and let A={i∈[K]:fi∗​(x~)>0}𝐴conditional-set𝑖delimited-[]𝐾subscriptsuperscript𝑓𝑖~𝑥0A=\{i\in[K]:f^{*}_{i}(\widetilde{x})>0\}. Then∑i∈[K]fi∗​(x~)subscript𝑖delimited-[]𝐾subscriptsuperscript𝑓𝑖~𝑥\displaystyle\sum_{i\in[K]}f^{*}_{i}(\widetilde{x})=∑i∈Afi∗​(x~)=∑i∈Asupxi∈spt​(μi){ψi​(xi)−c​(xi,x~)}.absentsubscript𝑖𝐴subscriptsuperscript𝑓𝑖~𝑥subscript𝑖𝐴subscriptsupremumsubscript𝑥𝑖sptsubscript𝜇𝑖subscript𝜓𝑖subscript𝑥𝑖𝑐subscript𝑥𝑖~𝑥\displaystyle=\sum_{i\in A}f^{*}_{i}(\widetilde{x})=\sum_{i\in A}\sup_{x_{i}\in\textup{spt}(\mu_{i})}\left\{\psi_{i}(x_{i})-c(x_{i},\widetilde{x})\right\}.Recalling (3.3),∑i∈Ac(xi,x~)≥cA(xi:i∈A).\sum_{i\in A}c(x_{i},\widetilde{x})\geq c_{A}(x_{i}:i\in A).Combined with the fact that ∑i∈Aψi(xi)≤1+cA(xi:i∈A)\sum_{i\in A}\psi_{i}(x_{i})\leq 1+c_{A}(x_{i}:i\in A), it follows that∑i∈[K]fi∗(x~)≤supxi∈spt​(μi):i∈A{∑i∈Aψi(xi)−cA(xi:i∈A)}≤1,\sum_{i\in[K]}f^{*}_{i}(\widetilde{x})\leq\sup_{x_{i}\in\textup{spt}(\mu_{i}):i\in A}\left\{\sum_{i\in A}\psi_{i}(x_{i})-c_{A}(x_{i}:i\in A)\right\}\leq 1,which verifies that f∗superscript𝑓f^{*} is feasible for (3.12) under the assumption that it is measurable.

Now, we will show that (f∗,μ~∗)superscript𝑓superscript~𝜇(f^{*},\widetilde{\mu}^{*}) is a saddle point for problem (2.7). Now we can prove (4.15). The first inequality of (4.15) is straightforward, since the definition of Bμ∗superscriptsubscript𝐵𝜇B_{\mu}^{*} in (2.8) and the optimality of μ~∗superscript~𝜇\widetilde{\mu}^{*} imply thatB​(f,μ~∗)+C​(μ,μ~∗)𝐵𝑓superscript~𝜇𝐶𝜇superscript~𝜇\displaystyle B(f,\widetilde{\mu}^{*})+C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu}^{*})≤supf∈ℱ{B​(f,μ~∗)+C​(μ,μ~∗)}=Bμ∗=B​(f∗,μ~∗)+C​(μ,μ~∗).absentsubscriptsupremum𝑓ℱ𝐵𝑓superscript~𝜇𝐶𝜇superscript~𝜇superscriptsubscript𝐵𝜇𝐵superscript𝑓superscript~𝜇𝐶𝜇superscript~𝜇\displaystyle\leq\sup_{f\in\mathcal{F}}\left\{B(f,\widetilde{\mu}^{*})+C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu}^{*})\right\}=B_{\mu}^{*}=B(f^{*},\widetilde{\mu}^{*})+C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu}^{*}).

Therefore, (f∗,μ~∗)superscript𝑓superscript~𝜇(f^{*},\widetilde{\mu}^{*}) is a saddle point for (2.7), hence for (2.6) and (1.1) also.∎

This follows immediately from the fact that the cost function 𝐜𝐜\mathbf{c} is invariant under permutations and the fact that all marginals of π∗superscript𝜋\pi^{*} are the same.∎

Through this section, the cost c𝑐c is as in Example 2.3. This cost has been widely used in adversarial learning literature and distributional robust optimization literature. Examples in this section illuminate how our general framework of generalized barycenter and MOT finds applications in practice.

Consider the binary case K=2𝐾2K=2. Our goal is to show that our results recover the result in [GM22].

In fact, the optimal adversarial attacks μ~~𝜇\widetilde{\mu} and the optimal barycenter measure λ𝜆\lambda depend on not only the geometry of the support of μ𝜇\mu but also the magnitudes of its marginals, μisubscript𝜇𝑖\mu_{i}’s.

In this section we illustrate our theoretical results numerically. We obtain robust classifiers for synthetic data sets and compute optimal adversarial risks for two popular real data sets: MNIST and CIFAR.

From the perspective of numeric, our aim is to solve the MOT problem (1.2) and its dual for an empirical measure μ𝜇\mu whose support consists of n𝑛n data points. We use Sinkhorn algorithm for concreteness. Introduced in [Cut13], Sinkhorn algorithm has been one of the central algorithmic tools in computational optimal transport in the past decade. This algorithm, originally introduced in the context of standard (222-marginal) optimal transport problems, was extended to MOTs in [BCC+15, BCN19]. Works that study the computational complexity of generic MOT problems include: [LHCJ22, TDGU20, HRCK21, Car22]. In particular, [LHCJ22] and [TDGU20] prove the complexity of MOT Sinkhorn algorithm to be O~​(K3​nK​ϵ−2)~𝑂superscript𝐾3superscript𝑛𝐾superscriptitalic-ϵ2\widetilde{O}(K^{3}n^{K}\epsilon^{-2}) and O~​(K3​nK+1​ϵ−1)~𝑂superscript𝐾3superscript𝑛𝐾1superscriptitalic-ϵ1\widetilde{O}(K^{3}n^{K+1}\epsilon^{-1}), respectively, where ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon is the error tolerance.

In our first illustration, we consider a data set (x1,y1),…​(xn,yn)subscript𝑥1subscript𝑦1…subscript𝑥𝑛subscript𝑦𝑛(x_{1},y_{1}),\dots(x_{n},y_{n}) in ℝ2×{1,2,3}superscriptℝ2123{\mathbb{R}}^{2}\times\{1,2,3\} obtained by sampling yisubscript𝑦𝑖y_{i} uniformly from {1,2,3}123\{1,2,3\} and then xisubscript𝑥𝑖x_{i} from a certain Gaussian distribution with parameters depending on the outcome of yisubscript𝑦𝑖y_{i}. We consider the cost c=cε𝑐subscript𝑐𝜀c=c_{\varepsilon} from Example 2.3 with d𝑑d the Euclidean distance in ℝ2superscriptℝ2{\mathbb{R}}^{2} and different values of ε𝜀\varepsilon. In Figure 5 we show the labels assigned to the data by the (approximate) robust classifier, which we computed using Corollary 4.7 for the dual potentials ϕjsubscriptitalic-ϕ𝑗\phi_{j} generated by the MOT Sinkhorn algorithm.

In our second illustration, we use the mutlimarginal version of Sinkhorn algorithm to compute the adversarial risk Rμ∗subscriptsuperscript𝑅𝜇R^{*}_{\mu} (i.e. the optimal value of (1.1)) for μ𝜇\mu an empirical measure supported on a subset of either the CIFAR or MNIST data sets. In both cases we consider samples belonging to one of four possible classes in order to decrease the computational complexity of the problem. We use the cost c𝑐c from Example 2.3 for different values of ε𝜀\varepsilon and two choices of d𝑑d: the Euclidean distance ℓ2superscriptℓ2\ell^{2} and the ℓ∞superscriptℓ\ell^{\infty} distance. The results are shown in Figure 6. We can observe that for the CIFAR data set the two distance functions behave similarly: while not the same, the plots exhibit a similar qualitative behavior. For the MNIST data set, on the other hand, the situation is markedly different: in contrast to the plot for the ℓ2superscriptℓ2\ell^{2} distance, the adversarial risk with ℓ∞superscriptℓ\ell^{\infty} distance varies dramatically as ε𝜀\varepsilon grows. This observation is consistent with the findings in [PJ21a] for the binary case.

We emphasize that Figure 6 only provides approximations of the true adversarial risk Rμ∗subscriptsuperscript𝑅𝜇R^{*}_{\mu}. Indeed, recall that Rμ∗=1−Bμ∗subscriptsuperscript𝑅𝜇1subscriptsuperscript𝐵𝜇R^{*}_{\mu}=1-B^{*}_{\mu}. Approximating Bμ∗subscriptsuperscript𝐵𝜇B^{*}_{\mu} using the MOT Sinkhorn algorithm will always produce an upper bound for Bμ∗subscriptsuperscript𝐵𝜇B^{*}_{\mu} since the regularization term effectively restricts the solution space of (1.2). Thus, the multimarginal Sinkhorn algorithm always yields a lower bound for the true Rμ∗subscriptsuperscript𝑅𝜇R^{*}_{\mu}. Of course, one can always compute a tighter lower bound by reducing the regularization parameter η𝜂\eta at the expense of increasing the computational burden.

As way of conclusion for this section we provide pointers to the literature discussing the computational complexity of the Wasserstein barycenter problem; Wasserstein barycenter problems are specific instances in the MOT family. On the one hand, [ABA22] prove certain computational hardness of the barycenter problem in the dimension of the feature space (here 𝒳𝒳{\mathcal{X}}). On the other hand, [ABA21] present an algorithm that can get an approximate solution of the optimal barycenter in polynomial time for a fixed dimension of the feature space. While our MOT is not the standard barycenter problem, it is still a generalized version thereof, and thus, it is reasonable to expect that the structure of our problem can be used in the design of algorithms that perform better than off-the-shelf MOT solvers. We leave this task for future work.

In this paper we have discussed a series of equivalent formulations of adversarial problems in the context of multiclass classification. These formulations take the form of problems in optimal transport, specifically, multimarginal optimal transport and (generalized) Wasserstein barycenter problem. Besides providing a novel connection between apparently unrelated fields, we have also discussed a series of theoretical and computational implications emanating from these equivalences. In what follows we briefly expand this discussion, while at the same time provide a few perspectives on future work.

First, it is of interest to design scalable algorithms for solving the MOT problem (1.2). In general, MOT problems are not scalable in the number of marginals of the problem. However, this may not necessarily be an issue for our MOT problem, since it possesses a special structure that, as we discussed throughout section 3, allows us to interpret the desired MOT problem as a generalized barycenter problem; barycenter problems, at least in their standard version, are known to scale much better than general MOT problems. Tailor-specific algorithms for our MOT problem can take advantage of the favourable geometric structure of a given data set. Indeed, if a data set is such that there is only a small number of classes (much smaller than K𝐾K) that interact with each other at the scale implicitly specified by the cost c𝑐c (think of Example 2.3), then the effective size of problem (1.2) will be considerably smaller than the size of the worst case setting —see the reformulation (3.6).

Second, it would be of interest to use (1.1) to help in the training of robust classifiers within specific families of models. Notice that (1.1) is model free from the perspective of the learner, but in applications practitioners may be interested in solving a problem like:inff∈𝒢supμ~∈𝒫​(𝒵){R​(f,μ~)−C​(μ,μ~)},subscriptinfimum𝑓𝒢subscriptsupremum~𝜇𝒫𝒵𝑅𝑓~𝜇𝐶𝜇~𝜇\displaystyle\inf_{f\in\mathcal{G}}\sup_{\widetilde{\mu}\in\mathcal{P}(\mathcal{Z})}\left\{R(f,\widetilde{\mu})-C(\mu,\widetilde{\mu})\right\},which differs from (1.1) in the family of classifiers 𝒢𝒢\mathcal{G}, which may be strictly smaller than ℱℱ\mathcal{F}; for example, 𝒢𝒢\mathcal{G} could be a family of neural networks, kernel-based classifiers, or other popular (parametric) models. There are two ways in which problem (1.1) is still meaningful for the above model-specific problem: 1) the optimal μ~∗superscript~𝜇\widetilde{\mu}^{*} computed from the problem (1.1) can be used as a way to generate adversarial examples that could be used during training of the desired model; 2) the optimal value of (1.1) can serve as a benchmark for robust training within any family of models.

Third, more theoretical understanding of optimal dual potentials and robust classifiers is required. As stated in Corollary 4.7, an optimal robust classifier can be obtained from solutions to (2.12), or, equivalently, from (3.13) and (3.14). However, unless c𝑐c is continuous, even the existence of Borel measurable dual potentials is not guaranteed, and hence neither is the existence of optimal robust classifiers. At this stage, it is thus necessary to assume that the classifier intorduced in Corollary 4.7 is Borel measurable. This measurability issue, i.e., that a robust classifier may not be Borel measurable, has been discussed not only in the adversarial training community [PJ21b, AFM21a, AFM21b, Fra22, FNW22], but also more generally in the distributional robust optimization community, e.g., [BM19]. In general, at this point only the existence of universally measurable robust classifier f∗superscript𝑓f^{*} can be guaranteed. Whether there exist Borel measurable robust classifiers for discontinuous costs (like the one in Example (2.3)) is a question that we hope to explore in future work.

Finally, it is of interest to investigate the geometric content that profiles like the ones presented in Figure 6 carry about a specific data set. As illustrated in Figure 6, these curves are specific signatures (adversarial signatures) of a given data distribution, and thus, they may be potentially used to capture similarities and discrepancies between different data sets.

The above are just but a few directions currently under investigation that emanate from this work.
